You should ignore your kids.
October 29, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äç

It‚Äôs almost impossible to stay on top of AI. It seems like every day there‚Äôs something crazy happening.
‚Äã
My new favorite podcast makes it easy. It‚Äôs called AI For Humans and aside from providing a deep dive into the latest AI news, it‚Äôs absolutely freaking hilarious.
‚Äã
I laugh my head off every single episode, the hosts, Kevin Pereira and Gavin Purcell could easily pass for professional comedians.
‚Äã
Highly recommend. Go check it out. Spotify / YouTube / Apple Podcasts‚Äã
‚Äã

How to be a great parent by ignoring your kids.
‚Äã
‚ÄãI found myself scratching my head while listening to a recent episode of The Daily, all about the misery of modern parenting.
‚Äã
When did raising children become so exhausting? According to the podcast, 71% of parents report that parenting is more stressful than ever before. So much so that even the U.S. Surgeon General has declared parenting a significant health risk.
‚Äã
‚ÄãLet‚Äôs take a minute to let that set in.
‚Äã
‚ÄãA role that is foundational to humanity‚Äôs very existence‚Äîalongside eating and sleeping‚Äîis now so burdened with stress that it‚Äôs become a mental health crisis.
‚Äã
In fact, modern parenting makes us so miserable that an increasing number of people are deciding not to have children at all.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve seen this firsthand: when I speak to people under 30, many of them are opting out of parenthood entirely.
‚Äã
Sometimes it‚Äôs due to concerns about climate change, but more often, it‚Äôs because they‚Äôve seen others struggle with parenthood and decided it doesn‚Äôt seem appealing.
‚Äã
‚ÄãFair enough. Modern parenting is a sad existence for many.
‚Äã
‚ÄãWe modern parents have calendars that look like a game of Tetris. 4:00 PM soccer practice, 5:30 PM piano lessons, 6:45 PM math tutoring, weekend slots filled with never-ending dance recitals and birthday parties. We transition from an autonomous child-free existence to a tiny human‚Äôs full-time taxi driver, personal assistant, therapist, and security detail.
‚Äã
We live in constant fear of traumatizing our kids, as if the slightest lapse in parental calm could spiral into a lifetime of intravenous drug use.
‚Äã
We‚Äôre consumed with guilt for using daycare, hiring help, or even taking time for ourselves, as if involving others in caregiving somehow diminishes our role or negatively impacts our children‚Äôs development.
‚Äã
And we hover at playgrounds, ensuring our delicate children are saved from any unnecessary bumps or scrapes.
‚Äã
Opportunities for work, hobbies, or simply having adult conversations are put aside‚Äîall sacrificed at the altar of ‚Äòperfect‚Äô parenting.
‚Äã
‚ÄãNo wonder parents are so unhappy.
‚Äã
‚ÄãAnd why?
‚Äã
‚ÄãBecause it‚Äôs completely unnatural.‚Äã
‚Äã
‚ÄãHumans are happiest when they live in alignment with their instincts.
‚Äã
Think about what makes us feel great - the experiences that light up our brains with feel-good chemicals like serotonin and oxytocin:
‚Äã
- Deep friendships forged through shared experiences
‚Äã
- Intimate connections and romance
‚Äã
- The pleasure of a meal shared with loved ones
‚Äã
- The rush of physical movement and play
‚Äã
- The belonging that comes from being part of a community
‚Äã
- The grounding effect of time spent in nature
‚Äã
It's no coincidence that these are the things our brains are designed for, and continue to make us happy. We're running the same "hardware" (our brains) as our hunter gatherer ancestors, so it's no surprise that we enjoy the same things that they would have done.
‚Äã
It just feels right to lift heavy objects, wander around in nature, eat tasty food, and laugh with friends‚Äîthe very things that are being sacrificed in modern parenting.
‚Äã
‚ÄãWhat‚Äôs wild is that this is an altogether new phenomenon. Just a few decades ago, children roamed their neighborhoods unaccompanied until dinner.
‚Äã
‚ÄãParents were preoccupied with work, social activities, and sports. The idea was that kids could entertain themselves.
‚Äã
My generation was one of the last to experience this: In the early 90s, by the time I was around eight years old, I was completely free range. No cell phone. No parents watching. Just me and my buddies riding around in a pack on our bikes throwing rocks at stuff. I‚Äôm grateful I got to experience this‚Äîbut I worry that unless I fight for it, my own children won‚Äôt have this same level of freedom, autonomy, and the confidence that comes with it.
‚Äã
Based on what we know about hunter gatherer societies, caregiving was communal, with adults sharing the responsibility for watching over children, a system known as ‚Äúalloparenting‚Äù. Studies of groups like the !Kung San and Hadza show that older children often cared for younger ones, allowing parents more time for work and leisure. This communal approach promoted social learning and independence among children, while reducing the parenting burden on any one adult. Kids ran around in a pack, with the older kids taking care of the younger ones, while the parents hung out, went hunting, and all that other Hunter Gatherer-y stuff.
‚Äã
There was no hovering or formalized instruction. Adults did their own thing and left the kids to their own devices unless safety concerns arose, allowing them to learn from their own mistakes and build independence. Children fit into their parents‚Äô lives, not the other way around.
‚Äã
‚ÄãThis communal, more relaxed approach worked for hundreds of thousands of years. But over the last few decades, something shifted.
‚Äã
‚ÄãHere‚Äôs a Before/After comparison:
‚Äã
‚Äã- Communal Parenting ‚ûû Two parents trapped in a lonely house with little kids
‚Äã
- Natural, Outdoor Play ‚ûû Structured indoor play with technology and educational toys
‚Äã
- Free-Range Exploration ‚ûû Supervised, scheduled playdates and activities
‚Äã
- Shared Childcare ‚ûû Childcare limited to parents and grandparents
‚Äã
- Intergenerational Interaction ‚ûû Nuclear family units with minimal extended family interaction
‚Äã
- Inherent Physical Activity ‚ûû Sedentary lifestyles with scheduled exercise
‚Äã
- Attachment to a Broad Group of Caregivers ‚ûû Focus on primary attachment to one or two parents
‚Äã
‚ÄãSo what changed?
‚Äã
‚ÄãIn the late 20th century, a shift occurred in the psyche of North American parents‚Äîa shift not rooted in science or logic, but in something far more insidious: fear.
‚Äã
The 1980s marked a turning point. The faces of missing children began appearing on milk cartons. Shows like America's Most Wanted turned rare tragedies into prime-time entertainment. Cable news discovered that stories of child abduction drove ratings, creating what sociologists now call "mean world syndrome" - the belief that the world is far more dangerous than statistics show. A generation of parents internalized these fears, despite living in what may be the safest time in human history to raise children.
‚Äã
‚ÄãConsider this truly insane fact:
‚Äã
‚ÄãThe statistical likelihood of your child being abducted by a stranger is about 1 in 1.5 million, according to the National Center for Missing & Exploited Children.
‚Äã
‚ÄãTo put that in perspective, your child is twice as likely to be struck by lightning, an event that hardly dictates our daily choices. Yet, the disproportionate fear of abduction has led parents to cocoon their children in layers of protection that earlier generations would find unrecognizable.
‚Äã
Ironically, studies have shown that this overprotective ‚Äòhelicopter‚Äô parenting hinders a child‚Äôs ability to navigate the world independently, leading to heightened anxiety and diminished problem-solving skills. A study published in the Journal of Child and Family Studies found that children granted less autonomy experienced higher stress levels and lower overall life satisfaction.
‚Äã
So, in our quest to shield our children from improbable dangers, we‚Äôre inadvertently exposing them to the very real risks of anxiety, dependency, and a diminished sense of self.
‚Äã
‚ÄãHow do we fix this in our own families?
‚Äã
‚ÄãImagine if I told you that the secret to losing weight was to eat more ice cream.
‚Äã
Well, this is one of those magical situations where the solution is actually to take the easy road.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs simple: give yourself a break. Stop over-scheduling, stop trying to constantly entertain and enrich your kids, and instead embrace what has worked for tens of thousands of years.
‚Äã
‚ÄãHere‚Äôs what I‚Äôve been doing in our household:
‚Äã
‚Äã- I‚Äôve stopped entertaining the boys‚ÄîI let them get bored and engage in creative play, ideally in the backyard. If they want to join in what I‚Äôm doing or help, that‚Äôs great. Of course, I‚Äôll step in if someone is badly injured, and we still have lots of nice family rituals (reading time, family dinner, sports, etc.), but most of the time, it‚Äôs their job to entertain themselves.
‚Äã
I‚Äôm always amazed by how, after a few minutes of whining, they disappear into a corner of the house, immersed in their own little world for hours.
‚Äã
- I‚Äôm resisting scheduling their time (one sport each‚Äîthat‚Äôs it).
‚Äã
- I‚Äôm inviting large groups of their friends over to recreate ‚Äúpacks‚Äù of kids. (I find I don‚Äôt hear from them for hours when we do this‚Äîthey completely occupy themselves.)
‚Äã
- I limit screens. (I do let them use iPads, but they‚Äôre strictly screen-timed, and once the timer is up, that‚Äôs it.)
‚Äã
- I bring them along with me to run errands. (They always complain at first, but end up having fun.)
‚Äã
- I try to focus on activities we both enjoy (versus standing around at some kid activity I hate.).
‚Äã
- I coordinate with other parents to pawn my children off for playdates at their house, or vice versa, and get together with other families to share meals as often as I can. (It‚Äôs amazing how much easier it is parenting as a group.)
‚Äã
- Soon, I‚Äôm going to start letting them bike to the park or play dates on their own. (I‚Äôm also working on getting more parents to agree to collectively send their kids to parks at the same time, so they aren‚Äôt doing this alone.
‚Äã
The evidence is clear: humans have successfully raised resilient, capable children for over 300,000 years without this anxiety-riddled misery. The solution to modern parenting isn't to parent harder - it's to parent lazier.
‚Äã
‚ÄãIf we don‚Äôt, the next generation will have two options:‚Äã
‚Äã
1. Live a semi-miserable, stress-filled existence for 18-years (have kids).
‚Äã
2. Never discover the wonders of raising a child, one of the great joys of life (no kids).
‚Äã
That‚Äôs a depressing thought, and potentially a real threat to humanity‚Äôs future.
‚Äã
So do your part. Join me in becoming a negligent parent (by today‚Äôs crazy standards) and embrace laziness!
‚Äã

Huberman says‚Ä¶
‚Äã
‚ÄãA few years ago, Tiny teamed up with Dr. Andrew Huberman to acquire Mateina, a Montreal-based company specializing in premium yerba mate.
‚Äã
Mateina‚Äôs loose-leaf and canned yerba mate have become a daily ritual for me, thanks to yerba mate‚Äôs health benefits and mellow caffeine high. (I find it much less anxiety-inducing than coffee, despite having over 120 mg of caffeine.)
‚Äã
Now, they‚Äôve made it even easier to enjoy yerba mate anytime, anywhere, with their latest innovation: yerba mate + electrolyte sticks.
‚Äã
Simply tear open a stick, stir it into hot or cold water, and you‚Äôll have a refreshing drink packed with naturally occurring caffeine. Plus, it‚Äôs infused with a custom blend of electrolytes formulated by Huberman himself, enhancing both the taste and the benefits. It‚Äôs perfect for travel or whenever you need a quick pick-me-up on the go.
‚Äã
‚ÄãGo buy some here‚Äã
‚Äã

I hate wealth managers.
‚Äã
‚ÄãMost of them add zero value and charge you a fortune to do something you could do yourself for free (build a diversified ETF portfolio).
‚Äã
And yet there‚Äôs so many of them.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs not logical, but people like having ‚Äúa guy‚Äù they trust. A trustworthy man or woman they met at the golf club who looks good in a suit and gives them a firm handshake.
‚Äã
Why? Because investing is kind of scary for most people.
‚Äã
You can explain ETFs and Peter Lynch (‚Äúbuy what you know‚Äù) and all that stuff, but ultimately, most people prefer having ‚Äúa guy‚Äù.
‚Äã
What sucks is, that person‚Äîusually a generically handsome tall man with salt and pepper hair and a name like Chase, Brock, or Preston‚Äîoften doesn‚Äôt know what the hell he‚Äôs talking about.
‚Äã
I can‚Äôt tell you how many times I've had these wealth management dudes pitch me, then watched them melt like a popsicle in death valley when I ask them the most basic investing questions.
‚Äã
Unfortunately, most of them are just sales people who sell you something you could easily buy yourself.
‚Äã
But I digress. Look‚Äîsome of you are just going to want ‚Äúa guy.‚Äù
‚Äã
So, I‚Äôd like to refer you to someone who I love.
‚Äã
My friend Jon Ronkai runs an accounting firm, and for almost 20 years he‚Äôs been investing his own money. He‚Äôs a dyed-in-the-wool value investor, and we actually met because we‚Äôre both part of a group of investors that meets monthly to talk about stocks. Over the years, I‚Äôve often nodded my head as he talked about an investment idea, and even followed him into a few investments.
‚Äã
Recently, he decided to start a wealth management division to service the clients of his accounting firm.
‚Äã
Finally, I have someone I can confidently refer people to‚Äîsomeone I genuinely believe is both smart and ethical.
‚Äã
So, if you need a guy, I recommend you speak to Jon Ronkai. I‚Äôd trust him with my life.
‚Äã
Email him here: jronkai@arbutusgroupcpa.com
‚Äã

My new favorite possession: a shotgun.
October 8, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äç

Every time I tweet about AeroPress, I get dozens of responses like this:
‚Äã
‚Äã"When are you going to make a non-plastic version?"
‚Äã
‚ÄãWell, we've finally done it.
‚Äã
Last week, we released the AeroPress Premium, a gorgeous all metal and glass version and it's phenomenal. I've been testing one for the last month and I absolutely love it.
‚Äã
Apparently, the world agrees, because we sold out in less than 3 hours.
‚Äã
You can pre-order one in time for the holidays here.
‚Äã

How business made me twenty best friends
‚Äã
‚ÄãIn 1938, the Harvard Grant Study began following over 700 men. They came from a variety of walks of life. Some would go on to become successful leaders, including John F. Kennedy, who would later become the 35th President of the United States. Others faced hardships, becoming homeless, or struggling with mental health issues.
‚Äã
For eighty-six years, researchers have been following this cohort, interviewing them extensively. Doing medical examinations. Tracking things like physical health, mental well-being, and career progression. Even interviewing their wives, children, and grandchildren.
‚Äã
‚ÄãThe findings are fascinating:
‚Äç
Good relationships make us healthier and happier.
Loneliness is as deadly as smoking or alcoholism.
Wealth and fame don't buy happiness‚Äîrelationships do.
Drugs and alcohol derail lives, ruin health, and strain relationships.

To summarize: it's all about relationships. Marriage. Friends. Community.
‚Äã
And maybe stop drinking too.
‚Äã
‚ÄãThis summer, I read The Good Life, a new book by Robert Waldinger, the study's current director, and it drove this home. As I sat on the dock of my lakeside cabin, listening to my kids play on the beach, I pondered my relationships.
‚Äã
I had a wonderful girlfriend. A great relationship with my two boys. A core group of close friends, and a huge extended network of acquaintances. But oddly, some of my deepest friendships had come from...business?
‚Äã
‚ÄãWhy? Because I'd stumbled into group therapy, without ever intending to.
‚Äã
‚ÄãFifteen years ago, I joined the Entrepreneur's Organization. As part of joining it, you are inducted into a "Forum".
‚Äã
Every month, you spend 3-4 hours in a conference room with six entrepreneurs. In it, you share your personal, family, and business problems. Highs and lows. What's going well, and what's not. It's the same group every time, and it's 100% confidential.
‚Äã
In my group, everyone was initially quite guarded, sticking to business topics and mostly purporting to be doing great. But as trust grew over many months, conversations went deeper. People began sharing not just business challenges, but also the struggles and triumphs in their personal and family lives.
‚Äã
Initially, I wasn't sure what I could learn from this group. We all ran such different businesses. Most weren't even in tech. In my first group, the other entrepreneurs ran businesses completely different from my own: a puzzle company, a woodworking shop, a popsicle company, and a mapping company. But, as my friend Brent Beshores says, it turned out, "in business, everything tastes like chicken".
‚Äã
‚ÄãOur problems were strangely universal. Over the years, we helped each other navigate:
‚Äç
Failed partnerships
Bankruptcies
Divorces
Fraud
Health scares
Toxic employees
Mental health struggles
Family deaths
Challenges raising children
‚Äç
Some of these forum mates have become my closest friends‚Äîso close that when I needed executors for my will, I chose them without hesitation.
‚Äã‚Äã
‚ÄãFor the past decade, I've run multiple groups, meeting nearly every week. I no longer do it through EO‚ÄîI run them myself. I have four forums, dedicating one evening a week to each. It's a rhythm that keeps me sane. Like a small, secular church group‚Äîa weekly space for real connection. A guarantee that I'll get a chance to socialize, laugh, and get some stuff off my chest.
‚Äã
There's something profound about sitting in a room, tackling shared struggles, and building trust over a decade. Forum has forged deeper friendships than any other part of my life‚Äîdeeper than high school, college, sports, or meeting parents at my kids' school. In 15 years, it's given me over 20 incredible friendships I never expected.
‚Äã
If you're an entrepreneur‚Äîor even if you‚Äôre not‚ÄîI can‚Äôt recommend starting a forum enough. I‚Äôve written a guide to help you get started. According to The Harvard Grant study, it could improve your life immensely. Get the guide here‚Äç
‚Äç‚Äã

My new favorite possession: A Shotgun
‚Äã
‚ÄãI recently solved a very silly problem for myself. When I let my dog out to pee in our backyard, I often leave the back door open and mosquitos and flys buzz into the house.
‚Äã
We have high ceilings, so big, fat flies often spend days buzzing around our heads as we try to swat at them. Sometimes, out of frustration, I'll grab a towel or book and hurl it at the ceiling, but it almost never works.
‚Äã
Enter, the Bug-A-Salt. I found this thing on YouTube and it's amazing. It's an air-powered shotgun that shoots table salt.
‚Äã
Pour in a teaspoon of table salt. Cock it. Aim at the bug. And boom, dead bug.
‚Äã
Be careful, by the way. You'll shoot your eye out!
‚Äã
You can buy them on Amazon.
‚Äç

Therapy for Nerds
‚Äã
‚ÄãI've spent years in talk therapy on and off. I've tried it all: CBT, ACT, Jungian, Internal Family Systems.
‚Äã
It's been really helpful in working through many issues. But I've often found myself craving a system.
‚Äã
As a business and process nerd, I wanted something repeatable. When X happens, do Y.
‚Äã
This year, I started doing Dialectical Behavior Therapy and it has really resonated for his exact reason. It's like therapy for business nerds: everything is just a flow chart.
‚Äã
You feel upset with your partner? Overwhelmed? Irritated? At a loss?
‚Äã
DBT gives you a system of next actions to take for each.
‚Äã
Here's two principles I like:
‚Äã
‚ÄãDo the Opposite - Break negative behavior by doing the opposite of what you feel. Follow these steps:
‚Äç
Identify the Urge - Notice the negative behavior you want to engage in. For instance, isolating yourself when feeling overwhelmed or lashing out verbally.
Do the Opposite Action - Actively choose to do the opposite of that urge.
Observe the Effect - Notice how doing the opposite affects your mood. Often, taking positive actions can change your emotions over time.

Emotional Regulation - Manage emotions in a healthy way by following these steps:
‚Äç
Assess the Situation - Identify what you're feeling and why. Is there an external trigger or internal thought causing the reaction?
Check Basics - Ask yourself if you've slept enough, eaten well, or are dehydrated. Physical needs can significantly impact emotions.
Breathing and Mindfulness - Take deep breaths or practice mindfulness to calm your initial emotional response.
Distract or Step Back - If you're still feeling overwhelmed, distract yourself with a different activity or take a break from the situation to gain perspective.
Physical Intensity - If emotions remain intense, engage in vigorous exercise or take a cold shower to physically alter your state.
Use Medication if Appropriate - If you're prescribed medication, consider if it's the right time to take it (e.g., anti-anxiety meds).
‚Äç
DBT is not holistic therapy. It's not going to help you work through childhood trauma or contemplate your family history. It's simply a model, for how to snap yourself out of unproductive habits.
‚Äã
I've found it really helpful and the "do the opposite" principle has definitely saved me from snapping in a few situations.
‚Äã
You can find therapist who practices DBT in your area on the Psychology Today database here.
‚Äã

A few years ago, I was going through a really hard time.
‚Äã
‚ÄãI was feeling listless and confused about what to do next.
‚Äã
I happened to be in New Zealand for a conference, and remembered that my friend Derek Sivers lived in Wellington, so I looked him up and we grabbed coffee.
‚Äã
The next hour, was one of the most impactful of my life.
‚Äã
For those who don't know Derek, he is one of the most thoughtful and interesting people I've ever met. An entrepreneurial Yoda who lives life according to his own rule book.
‚Äã
I recounted that story in one of the chapters of my book, Never Enough.
‚Äã
Recently, Derek and I spent an hour chatting. This time for my podcast. And as always, he was absolutely fascinating.
‚Äã
‚ÄãWe talk about:
‚Äç
Why he gave away his $22 million dollar fortune.
Why he's an explorer not a leader.
How he has "burned the ships" over and over again.
His annual ritual for reversing prejudices.
‚Äç
And so much more.
‚Äã
Check it out: YouTube, Apple, Spotify.
‚Äã‚Äã

Are you a rat or a frog?
September 10, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã

I get distracted easily. If I‚Äôm not careful, I‚Äôm easily led down rabbit trails and become unfocused across 20 browser tabs. Oddly, despite being easily distracted, I find that I work best from cafes.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve always found the silence of a closed-door office overwhelming. Over the last twenty years, I‚Äôve tried everything from fancy private offices, to buzzy open-concept spaces, to co-working, but nothing hits quite like cafes.
‚Äã
In order to stay focused in a noisy environment, I rely on some guardrails to stay on track. Cafes can be loud and distracting, so I‚Äôve always used my AirPods Pro to drown out the noise. Recently, I discovered a way to make this experience 10x better.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve discovered an app that I absolutely love. It‚Äôs called Endel and it creates the perfect soundscape for whatever you‚Äôre doing. Ranging from work (deep work, afternoon energy drift, colored noise), to reading, to walking, it dynamically creates the perfect soundtrack to your day (even going so far as to use your heart rate from your Apple Watch to switch it up ‚Äî super cool).
‚Äã
There‚Äôre a million apps like this, but Endel is by far the best designed and executed. Go check it out
‚Äã

Are you a rat or a frog? Zoe told me about this bizarre meme: all people can be divided into two groups: rats and frogs.
‚Äã
As soon as you think about it, you‚Äôll start laughing. Just start naming people you know and your gut will immediately tell you if they‚Äôre a rat or a frog.
‚Äã
Me: Rat‚Äã
Chris: Frog‚Äã
Zoe: Frog‚Äã
One of my sons: Frog‚Äã
The other one: Rat‚Äã
‚Äã
I can‚Äôt explain it, but it cracks me up. Try it, it‚Äôs hilarious.
‚Äã

Who knew being a trader at JP Morgan was so sexy and exotic? It‚Äôs not, but the writers of Industry would have you think it is.
‚Äã
Regardless, it‚Äôs a blast to watch, and definitely one of my favorite shows from the last few years. The third season just started and I‚Äôm loving it. It‚Äôs not dissimilar to Billions: lots of silly exaggeration and drama added to one of the world‚Äôs dullest jobs, but the cast is incredible and it‚Äôs a lot of fun to watch. It‚Äôs on HBO Max (US) or Crave (Canada).
‚Äã

I‚Äôm hosting an event for lonely conglomerateurs (yes, that is a word ü§¢, and if you ever want to get punched in the face, introduce yourself as one).
‚Äã
‚ÄãThere are tons of support groups for executives and CEOs, but not for folks who run holding companies. It‚Äôs a unique role with a rare structure and set of challenges. And frankly, it‚Äôs often pretty lonely because there are so few people who can relate to your problems (of which there are many: perverse incentives, compensation negotiations, reporting complexities, etc).
‚Äã
In fact, I so rarely meet people who I can talk to about these problems with that I‚Äôve decided to do what I always do. If I can‚Äôt find my group, I build my own group.
‚Äã
So, in January, I‚Äôm hosting the first of what I hope to make a bi-annual event exclusively for people who run at-scale holding companies. It‚Äôll be hyper confidential, YPO forum format, and we‚Äôll have folks from a variety of industries and scales. It will be very intimate, with less than 30 people attending.
‚Äã
We‚Äôre still working out the details, but if you‚Äôre interested in coming email monica@tiny.com with:
A quick bio (if you have one)
A link to your site
How many companies you own
Total revenue (range is fine)
Where you‚Äôre based
‚Äã

My friend Codie Sanchez came on the pod. I love her message: the best way to get rich is boring and under your nose. It's not AI or drone tech‚Äîit's traditional, old school businesses run by baby boomers. I loved this conversation. Check it out here.
‚Äã

An innovative use for rectal thermometers.
August 30, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã‚Äã

‚ÄúAny sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic.‚Äù
‚Äã
‚ÄãI‚Äôve always loved this quote by Arthur C. Clarke.
‚Äã
Think back to the first time you saw an iPhone or asked ChatGPT a question.
‚Äã
It felt like pure, unadulterated magic, didn‚Äôt it? If you had traveled back in time and shown someone these technologies, they would probably burn you at the stake.
‚Äã
Yet today, we use both without a second thought.
‚Äã
I still have moments with ChatGPT where I gasp in awe‚Äîlike when it effortlessly analyzes 10 years of a company‚Äôs financial statements in seconds, or crafts a flawless paragraph that puts my own to shame. But mostly, it‚Äôs just become another tool in the toolkit.
‚Äã
I‚Äôm having one of these moments right now, with self-driving cars.
‚Äã
If you‚Äôve been following along for a while, you might remember when I posted a photo of myself grinning like an idiot outside of a Waymo in San Francisco. I had summoned it with an Uber-like app, and moments later, this futuristic taxi rolled up, unlocking itself for us. Zoe and I sat in the back, in complete shock as it expertly navigated left turns and unpredictable traffic.
‚Äã
We couldn‚Äôt believe it. We shared videos with everyone we knew‚Äîit felt like magic.
‚Äã
But this morning, I got into my Tesla, punched in my favorite caf√© on the map, turned on AutoPilot, and leaned back to listen to a podcast.
‚Äã
I was completely unfazed. Self-driving has become my new normal. I rarely drive manually anymore. What was once astonishing has quickly become just another part of my day.
‚Äã
It reminds me of COVID. During the lockdowns, I remember thinking, ‚ÄúI‚Äôll never take being around people for granted again.‚Äù But once the restrictions were lifted, the elation lasted a few weeks before I returned to my baseline, taking human contact for granted once more.
‚Äã
Today, I‚Äôve almost forgotten what that isolation felt like.
‚Äã
Life goes on. But we shouldn‚Äôt forget how insanely amazing the modern world is.
‚Äã
A self-driving AI-powered robot car is not something that should be taken for granted.
‚Äã
‚ÄãPS: If you haven‚Äôt checked out Tesla‚Äôs Full Self-Driving technology yet, you should. It‚Äôs absolutely incredible. You don‚Äôt even need to hold the wheel anymore. As long as you‚Äôre paying attention and looking out the front windshield, it does everything. I frequently go 30-minutes or more without any intervention.
‚Äã

When I began writing Never Enough, I made a list of my favorite business books that I wanted to use as a benchmark for my own book.
‚Äã
‚ÄãAt the very top were two of my all-time favorites: Shoe Dog by Phil Knight and The Hard Thing About Hard Things by Ben Horowitz.
‚Äã
The other day, I received a surprising text from a friend: ‚ÄòCongrats on the review!‚Äô I had no idea what he was talking about.
‚Äã
Curious, I quickly Googled my name‚Äîand my jaw dropped. My book had been reviewed in none other than The Wall Street Journal. THE business newspaper.
‚Äã
I‚Äôm not ashamed to admit that I let out a shriek of pure joy.
‚Äã
I couldn‚Äôt believe my eyes when I read the final paragraph of the review by Daniel Freedman:
‚Äã‚Äã
‚ÄúNever Enough‚Äô succeeds in a way that only a handful of business autobiographies do. It shares with the best of them, like Phil Knight‚Äôs ‚ÄòShoe Dog‚Äô and Ben Horowitz‚Äôs ‚ÄòThe Hard Things About Hard Things,‚Äô the virtue of providing valuable lessons in an entertaining format, all while being honest about the writer‚Äôs own shortcomings and failures. Mr. Wilkinson joins his entrepreneurial achievements with an informative exploration of what they ought to be aimed at, in the end.‚Äù
‚Äã
‚ÄãMade me grin. You can read the review here.
‚Äã

I funded an important study by Dr. Ashley Mason at UCSF that involves sticking thermometers up people‚Äôs rectums.
‚Äã
‚ÄãSorry, that was the only way I could guarantee to keep you reading. But seriously, keep reading, this is really cool.
‚Äã
If you‚Äôre a Tech Bro‚Ñ¢ type or have ever listened to Huberman Lab or Dr. Rhonda Patrick, then you‚Äôre well versed in the benefits of saunas and cold plunge, but it turns out that high temperature saunas may treat severe treatment resistant depression.
‚Äã
Researchers have long known that depression is often accompanied by a slight but consistent increase in body temperature. The question that intrigued scientists was whether changing that temperature could, in turn, alter the course of the depression.
‚Äã
Enter the sauna. Anecdotal evidence has suggested for years that regular sauna use can boost well-being, but only recently have researchers begun to look seriously at its potential to treat depression.
‚Äã
In the study, for 8 weeks, participants with major depressive disorder were fitted with a rectal thermometers (unfortunately the most accurate way to measure body temperature when in a sauna) and exposed to extremely long (140 minute) weekly sauna sessions that raised their body temperature to a feverish temperature of 101.3 F.
‚Äã
What was incredible was that participants saw their symptoms significantly improve after just a few weeks of regular heat sessions. The effect was so pronounced that nearly all of them no longer met the criteria for major depressive disorder at the end of the trial.
‚Äã
This isn‚Äôt to say that saunas are a silver bullet for depression‚Äîfar from it. The studies so far have been small, and much more research is needed to confirm the results and understand the mechanisms at play. But the early findings are promising enough that researchers are calling for more attention and funding to explore this avenue further.
‚Äã
To date, we have committed over half a million dollars to this project, with more to come. If you‚Äôre interested in donating alongside us, please donate here (and make sure you specify you want the money to go to Dr. Ashley Mason): https://giving.ucsf.edu/fund/other
‚Äã
‚ÄãIf you‚Äôre curious to learn more, check out:
- Interview on Found My Fitness‚Äç
‚Äã- Talk at BrainMind Summit‚Äã
‚Äã

I used to be a terrible sleeper.
‚Äã
‚ÄãI was an inveterate night owl and would often spend hours trying to fall asleep, anxiously tossing and turning.
‚Äã
When I had kids, I had to get real and figure it out. And for the most part, I did. I posted my key sleep tips on X a few years ago (check them out here), but all this talk about Dr. Ashley Mason reminded me about a sleep tip she gave me that has been a game changer.
‚Äã
A few years ago, over lunch, she mentioned that she used to run the UCSF sleep clinic‚Äîthe place they send the worst of the worst, the insomniac‚Äôs insomniacs.
‚Äã
Curious, I asked her what the number one breakthrough was for her patients.
‚Äã
Her answer surprised me:
‚Äã
‚ÄãShe said most people simply sleep with a blanket that‚Äôs way too hot.‚Äã
‚Äã
Time and time again, she found that getting people to switch to a few thin woven cotton blankets (like this one‚Äîlayered so you can adjust based on temperature) had the greatest impact on improving their sleep.
‚Äã
Interestingly, your body temperature needs to drop by about 2 to 3 degrees Fahrenheit (or about 1 degree Celsius) for sleep to initiate effectively. A hot blanket can prevent this from happening.
‚Äã
I decided to try it for myself and found that I fall asleep much faster. In fact, it works so well that I (embarrassingly) pack one of these blankets to bring with me when I travel.
‚Äã

My first business was an agency, and running one is pure, unadulterated chaos‚Äîespecially in the early days.‚Äã
‚Äã
Why? Because the entire business is built around people. And as my business partner Chris puts it:
‚Äã
‚Äã‚ÄúThere‚Äôs no such thing as problems. Only people problems.‚Äù
‚Äã
‚ÄãAlmost daily, I receive emails or DMs from frazzled agency owners asking for advice on managing their businesses. I usually respond with a shrug.
‚Äã
The truth is, I haven‚Äôt been involved in day-to-day agency operations for years.
‚Äã
‚ÄãMetalab now has a fantastic executive team that has grown the company far beyond what I ever could. Honestly, if I worked there now, I‚Äôd probably get fired.
‚Äã
I was good at starting an agency but terrible at running one.
‚Äã
That‚Äôs why I brought in Mark‚Äîmy college roommate. You might remember the story from Chapter 4 of my book, but TLDR: Mark saved my bacon. He took over from me a few years in and grew Metalab into what it is today, leading it from 2009 to 2015.
‚Äã
Mark‚Äôs a mensch who truly knows his stuff when it comes to agencies.
‚Äã
Last year, he retired from agency life, but boredom got the best of him. Now, he‚Äôs decided to share some of his prophetic wisdom with agency owners through his consultancy, Tamaribuchi. If you‚Äôre struggling to take your agency to the next level, call Mark‚Äîhe‚Äôll set you straight.
‚Äã

That‚Äôs all for this week‚Ä¶

Never tell, always storytell.
August 16, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã‚Äã

Never tell, always storytell.
‚ÄãA few weeks ago, I invited 80 exceptional people to Victoria for my second annual Interesting People conference. This year, we focused on storytelling. We had Matt Dicks, who has won The Moth an absurd number of times and has the ability to make even the most innocuous story bring tears to any audience‚Äôs eyes.
‚Äã
Storytelling is a superpower I've honed over the last decade. Too many people screw up by just dumping facts:
‚Äã
‚Äã‚ÄúHere‚Äôs what my startup does. This is the market it‚Äôs tackling. Here‚Äôs the customer persona.‚Äù
‚Äã
‚ÄãBORING. I‚Äôm asleep already.
‚Äã
What you should be doing is telling your story. Sharing your hero‚Äôs journey. Talking about your fuck ups and the chip on your shoulder. Helping others see themselves in you, feeling bought in.
‚Äã
Matt was incredible, and in just under 48 hours we had multiple people get up onstage and tell incredible stories with a bit of coaching from Matt.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs not rocket science, and he breaks it all down in his new book on using stories in a business context. It‚Äôs called Stories Sell, and you should go check it out.
‚Äã

My friend Nick Gray MC‚Äôd the Interesting People conference. I think he‚Äôs one of the most interesting people I know, because instead of fixating on getting rich (which he has quietly become), he is focused on building a huge network of friends. He does this in an incredibly nerdy way, where he basically builds a funnel: ‚Äã
‚Äã
Social Media -> Events -> Smaller Dinners -> Close Friendships.
‚Äã
I just interviewed him for my podcast and he walks through it all.
‚Äã
If you‚Äôre feeling like you want more friends, then you need to check out this interview.
‚Äã
‚ÄãBillionaire < Friend Billionaire. Check out the interview here.‚Äã
‚Äç‚Äã

I often snap back to reality after 30 minutes of scrolling YouTube and wonder what happened. It‚Äôs my #1 time suck, and yet I don‚Äôt want to block it. It‚Äôs too useful.
‚Äã
So, the other day I discovered UnTrap for YouTube. It‚Äôs really simple: when you go to YouTube.com, it redirects you to your history or watch later page. The idea being that you can‚Äôt go down the rabbit hole and instead either have to search for what you specifically want, or just view stuff you‚Äôve previously saved to watch later.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve found it‚Äôs really helped me cut down on YouTube time. Highly recommended.
‚Äã

This year, a few simple negotiations made Tiny shareholders enough extra profit to buy six Porsche 911s.
‚Äã
‚ÄãIf you own your business, every dollar of savings is a dollar in your pocket.
‚Äã
Accordingly, you're insane not to negotiate all your expenses.
‚Äã
Often all it takes is asking: "Is that the best you can do?" or sharing a quote from a competitor.
‚Äã
Back in the day, Chris and I used to negotiate everything.
‚Äã
Office leases. Furniture. Software. Food for the office.
‚Äã
You name it, we haggled and saved millions.
‚Äã
But now Tiny is huge.
‚Äã
Now we only have time to negotiate the big stuff: incentive packages and M&A deals‚Äîhigh level capital allocation.
‚Äã
But the little stuff still matters. Our companies collectively spend tens of millions a year with companies like Google, Zoom, Amazon, Microsoft, and all the usual suspects.
‚Äã
We have over 40 companies and it's hard to incentivize employees to negotiate.
‚Äã
Because let's be real: negotiating is stressful.
‚Äã
So, this year, we started a new company to solve that problem. It's called TechSource and they do "Negotiation As A Service".
‚Äã
Sound familiar? It is. We started a similar business called Buyer with our friend Kimia a few years ago and he ended up selling it to Ramp.
‚Äã
But as soon as we sold it, we regretted it. We needed this service and our tech spend started creeping up again.
‚Äã
Now, we're back with TechSource.
‚Äã
They're 100% focused on negotiating one of tech companies' and startups' biggest P&L expense items:
‚Äã
Software.
‚Äã
Here's an example of what they did for Metalab:
‚Äã
They completely outsourced their software procurement process to TechSource and saved $72,000 in the first month and $264,000 annually in addition to saving employees over 40 hours of work that can now be allocated to billable projects.
‚Äã
Google: 27% savings
JamF: 40% savings
Drata: 85% savings
Zoom: 38% savings
‚Äã
That's pure profit to the bottom line. And they aren't even finished yet.
‚Äã
I freaking love this business. It's such a no brainer for any founder that spends a lot of money on infrastructure and software.
‚Äã
You literally just send them a breakdown of all your expenses and they get to work.
‚Äã
They're currently making their way through all of our companies and they've saved us over $600,000 so far.
‚Äã
Chat with Will Putnam if you're interested, you can email him here.
‚Äã
‚Äã
I had a lot of fun doing these two longform interviews:
‚Äç‚ÄãModern Wisdom with Chris Williamson‚Äç
‚ÄçBig Deal with Codie Sanchez
‚Äç‚Äã

THANK YOU.
Ok, back to our regularly scheduled newsletter where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week..

I work from cafes and hotel rooms a lot, and I often find myself hunching over my laptop in an awkward position. Then, my friend Codie told me about the Logitech Casa Pop-up Desk. It‚Äôs really cool. It looks like a little laptop case, and inside it has a bluetooth keyboard, a trackpad, and a laptop stand. In short: a desktop-like experience from anywhere you happen to be. I love mine and it‚Äôs become part of my day-to-day kit that I carry everywhere.
‚Äã

Time perception is a strange thing. When you‚Äôre in a routine, time rips by. Break the routine, and time slows down.
‚Äã
There are a bunch of different ideas about why this is, but the one that makes the most sense to me is the theory that your brain is constantly mapping out the world and building models. It‚Äôs a prediction engine, and the more it can predict the terrain/people/experience, the fewer new memories it needs to form.
‚Äã
I read an interesting article about this phenomenon a month or so ago and it recommended changing things up to extend your sense of time. Drive a different route to work. Rearrange your desk. Vary your experience as much as possible.
‚Äã
Most days, Zoe and I take the dog out for a walk in our neighborhood. We‚Äôd typically do the same route. In an effort to mix things up (and expand our sense of time), I downloaded an awesome app called AllTrails. Basically, it‚Äôs a crowdsourced map of all of the best hiking/walking paths and parks in your area. Through it I‚Äôve found dozens of parks I didn‚Äôt even know about, and we‚Äôve been trying new ones every day.
‚Äã
Oddly, this has added a sense of novelty to my life, I assume because my brain is building new models each time and therefore building more memories. It has made me oddly happy and become a fun new hobby: finding cool hidden natural beauty in my own city. So often, we think we need to travel to have new experiences, but sometimes the novelty is right under our noses.
‚Äã

Each summer, I try to disconnect from all the noisy stuff and focus on reading. I recently read two books that I absolutely loved, both for different reasons.
‚Äã
1. Useful Not True by Derek Sivers - Derek is one of my favorite writers, and his latest book is focused on a simple idea: our beliefs are often not necessarily true, but simply useful to us. That beliefs are tools, not truths. They should be judged by how useful they are, not by how true they are.
‚Äã
I read it in one sitting and absolutely loved it. In fact, I liked it so much I trained ChatGPT on the book and started asking it questions to help me work through a few problems.
‚Äã
I highly recommend it, it‚Äôs a 1-2 hour read and it slapped me in the face. I also recommend Derek's other books (How To Live is especially good).
‚Äã
2. Titan: The Life of John D. Rockefeller by Ron Chernow - I‚Äôm reading this one for the second time, and I‚Äôm loving it. Here‚Äôs what I‚Äôve found particularly interesting about it:
‚Äã
‚Ä¢ Rockefeller was a walking contradiction: a brutal monopolist, yet deeply honest, religious, and generous in the rest of his life. This makes for a fascinating character study.
‚Äã
‚Ä¢ The book offers valuable insights about philanthropy and how to do it effectively. Rockefeller single-handedly brought evidence-based medical research to the United States and generally gave to causes that compounded knowledge (universities/education/medical research). He was unique in his approach to giving: he did not seek to control or ask for credit, and largely left the non-profits he funded alone, even when he disagreed with them.
‚Äã
‚Ä¢ The story serves as a great reminder of Mark Twain's observation that "History doesn't repeat, but it does rhyme." Rockefeller's era faced concerns that echo today's issues: fears about a weak younger generation, the rise of socialism, and the growing power of unions.
‚Äã

I found a t-shirt that I love. It‚Äôs made by Ecolygist, a brand based here in Victoria, and it‚Äôs nice and thick, and fits perfectly. I just bought a whole bunch of them. Check it out here.
‚Äã

I recently spoke with Ali Abdaal, a doctor turned entrepreneur, and the world‚Äôs most followed productivity YouTuber. Think of it as a mini coaching session on everything I‚Äôve learned from starting businesses, hiring, delegating, making mistakes, and writing my book, Never Enough. Ali is one of the nicest people I have met in the past few weeks. Check out the full interview HERE.
‚Äã

I couldn't burp.
June 18, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã

Every day, I‚Äôd feel the burning. First, the pressure in my stomach would build. Then the right side of my throat would feel like someone had a blow torch to it.
‚Äã
It was acid reflux. And I had it almost every day for a decade. It started when I was about 25. Subtle, at first. I could take a Tums, and it would go away. No big deal. But before long, it became unbearable. The only thing that would prevent it was a ridiculously strict diet, and even then, I‚Äôd often have flare-ups.
‚Äã
What was odd about it was that the typical drugs did nothing. Tums made it better for 15 minutes, then it was back with a vengeance. And oddly, proton pump inhibitors, drugs that reduce stomach acid, made it twice as bad.
‚Äã
I was at a loss. Then, one day, I randomly stumbled on a Reddit community called ‚ÄúNoBurp.‚Äù
‚Äã
All my life, I‚Äôd never been able to burp, and whenever I‚Äôd mentioned it to a doctor I‚Äôd been brushed off: ‚ÄúYou‚Äôre burping, I promise you, you just don‚Äôt realize it.‚Äù
‚Äã
But I wasn‚Äôt. I‚Äôd only burped a few times in my life, often at random moments when I was laughing really hard. (Which was as embarrassing as you can imagine.)
‚Äã
It turned out that the people on Reddit all had an incredibly rare disorder called Retrograde Cricopharyngeal Dysfunction. Its primary symptom: the inability to burp, caused by an especially tight cricopharyngeal muscle. One of the most common symptoms: acid reflux and pressure in the throat after drinking carbonated beverages or a large meal.
‚Äã
I was intrigued.
‚Äã
It seemed whacky, but I thought maybe this could be it. And there was just one doctor in the world working on it. A guy named Dr. Robert Bastian, in Chicago. After a Reddit user suffering from this disorder contacted him, he decided to try an experimental procedure: injecting Botox into the patient‚Äôs throat using an endoscope. Within a few days, this patient, miraculously, could burp, and Dr. Bastian began performing this procedure on hundreds of others, with most gaining the ability to burp. Here‚Äôs a great little YouTube video about a guy going through it.
‚Äã
So in 2020 I flew to Chicago. Dr. Bastian and his team put me under, gave me the procedure, and within a day I was letting out little burps, then medium burps, then massive, room-shaking Homer Simpson burps. What was especially crazy was that I had absolutely no control over them, so they would erupt in the middle of a meeting or dinner out at a restaurant, which led to many awkward explanations.
‚Äã
Magically, my reflux almost completely disappeared, and three years later I‚Äôm still burping up a storm.
‚Äã
To help more folks navigate this bizarre and rare disorder, I created a simple website walking through it: www.noburp.org.
‚Äã
Can you burp? If not, you might want to take a trip to Chicago too.

‚Äã

My friend Brent Beshore did a really wonderful interview on The Knowledge Project and I found myself nodding along. In it, he talks about some of the maladaptive behaviors that made him a superhero at work but challenged his personal life and distanced him from others. He was seeking control in all situations. Always paying for the bill. Always being the loudest in the room. Always ensuring he controlled all the variables and had everything planned out.
‚Äã
A quote that struck me: ‚ÄúAre you optimizing your life for the people who know you best, or the people who know you least?‚Äù‚Äã
‚Äã
It reminded me a lot of some of the work I‚Äôve done on myself over the last few years, and I really loved the interview. Huge respect to Brent for being so open and helping others in the process.
‚Äã
‚ÄãListen here‚Äã
‚Äã

For years, I‚Äôve been searching for the perfect overnight travel bag. My pal Rob at Outway teamed up with Haven bags and cracked it. It looks like a simple backpack, but it‚Äôs secretly HUGE and insanely well-organized. Highly recommend it. I've been traveling all week and loving it.
‚Äã
‚ÄãCheck it out here‚Äã
‚Äã

I‚Äôve been loving Sam Parr‚Äôs new podcast, MoneyWise. In it, he goes deep (often anonymously) with wealthy people about their lives. How much they spend, what‚Äôs worth it, what isn‚Äôt. How money has impacted their lives and changed their relationships.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs really unique and something you so rarely hear people talk about. I particularly liked this episode with ‚ÄúJeff,‚Äù a guy who sold his software company for $120M, bought four houses and a jet, and then realized he was just as miserable as ever.
‚Äã
Great quote from this episode: ‚ÄúI‚Äôm really good at preparing to be happy, but not being happy‚Äù.
‚Äã
‚ÄãListen here‚Äã
‚Äã

I‚Äôm a big fan of Ugmonk. They make insanely beautiful desktop organizer systems for anal retentive nerds.

Delete 95% of your email.
June 5, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã

Today's edition is brought to you by Athena.

The last thing I want to do is spend time calling the optometrist to get my latest prescription.

Last year, I hired Tina, my Athena assistant, and tasks like this disappeared from view.

They just got done, magically, behind the scenes.

When Tina stepped in, she took over my inbox and calendar, as well as my life. She was like a military general, ensuring nothing fell through the cracks and everything stayed on track.

But her true value shone in unexpected ways. She handled all the things that would otherwise sidetrack me.

Coordinating car repairs. Keeping track of meeting notes. Coordinating legal documents.

The annoying little tasks that would otherwise eat away at my time.

I stopped having to think about any of it, so I could focus on what really mattered.

What‚Äôs magical about an Athena assistant, is they come pre-trained.

They know exactly what to do and come with opinions on how to improve your workflow. And somehow, they cost a fraction of a North American assistant.

What are you waiting for? Talk to Athena

‚Äç

‚Äç

Now onto this week‚Äôs issue‚Ä¶

‚Äç

You should ignore most problems.
‚Äã
‚ÄãThis morning, I left my phone in the car and took my dog for a walk in a wooded forest near my house.
‚Äã
Within a few minutes, I felt calmer and more present.
‚Äã
Interestingly, at that moment, all of my life‚Äôs problems still existed. Hundreds of unread emails and texts piling up. Podcasts I‚Äôve been meaning to listen to. Never-ending to-do lists.
‚Äã
I just wasn‚Äôt aware of any of it. It wasn‚Äôt in my face, blaring out of my trusty 6.7-inch portal into stress.
‚Äã
About six months ago, I used an app called Freedom to block all news and social media on my phone.
‚Äã
Now, when I try to click on news or open Instagram, Reddit, or X, my screen goes gray. I just can‚Äôt use any of it. Period.
‚Äã
I went from having an opinion on every stock market gyration and geopolitical event to not really being aware of any of it.
‚Äã
Sure, I was loosely up to speed. I‚Äôd hear mutterings about what was happening from friends. I‚Äôd read the occasional copy of The Wall Street Journal. I knew enough to not embarrass myself at dinner parties, but, mostly, I‚Äôd focus on other problems. Things that I could actually control.
‚Äã
I knew Trump got indicted, and why, but didn‚Äôt know the details. I figured I would find out what happened in the trial after it concluded, instead of doing what I used to do: obsessively follow every second of it minute-by-minute in The New York Times.
‚Äã
It felt like I knew enough to not have my head in the sand, but not so much that I was dwelling on it.
‚Äã
In retrospect, I think this is the right amount of attention for most of life‚Äôs problems. And it‚Äôs interesting how often stress simply comes from awareness.
‚Äã
Think about that in the context of the stock market vs. home ownership.
‚Äã
Most people say they ‚Äúcan‚Äôt stomach‚Äù owning stocks because they go up and down. It‚Äôs scary. They‚Äôre a roller-coaster.
‚Äã
What they don‚Äôt realize is that‚Äîif they own a home‚Äîthe same thing is happening to the value of their house. It‚Äôs just invisible to them.
‚Äã
They‚Äôre not aware of it. There‚Äôs no ticker for a house price, even though home prices whip-saw around month to month, year to year.
‚Äã
This lack of awareness doesn‚Äôt change the reality, but it does change the experience. You don‚Äôt stress over the daily fluctuations because you don‚Äôt see them. Your house might lose $50,000 in value one month and gain it back the next, but unless you‚Äôre trying to sell, it‚Äôs irrelevant to your daily life.
‚Äã
Lately, I‚Äôve been trying to remind myself to have as little awareness as possible of problems I don‚Äôt control and can‚Äôt impact, and as much calm space as I can from problems I do control. Yes, every once in a while I need to jump on an immediate grenade, but let‚Äôs be real‚Äîif I deleted 95% of my emails today, nothing bad would happen.
‚Äã

Jason Fried is my freaking hero. He‚Äôs built a massive company where he does exactly what he wants and runs it in a totally unconventional way. I‚Äôve been a fan of his for decades and his company 37signals was a huge inspiration for how we built many of our own companies. I interviewed him on the podcast and the episode just went live.
‚Äã
Watch on YouTube or tune in on Apple Podcasts or Spotify.
‚Äã

I‚Äôve spent the last decade reading the world‚Äôs greatest investors' annual letters. For those who don‚Äôt know, most public company and hedge fund CEOs write an annual (and sometimes quarterly) letter to their shareholders.
‚Äã
Most are boring as hell, verging on political fluff, but some are incredibly well written and informative. Warren Buffet being the most famous example. His letters are considered holy scriptures to value investors like myself, and it‚Äôs fascinating to start at the beginning of an investors career and see the progression (and compounding) over many decades. There‚Äôs a power to zooming out and seeing an investor‚Äôs long term trajectory. How their thinking changes over time. What stays the same. Which favorite investment in one letter turns out to be a blunder in another. The language they use to compliment (or covertly diss) management teams and competitors.
‚Äã
A few of my favorites that are worth checking out:
‚Äã
- David Einhorn from Greenlight Capital
- Bill Ackman from Pershing Square
- Joe Steinberg and Ian Cumming from Leucadia
- Mark Leonard from Constellation Software
- Prem Watsa from Fairfax
- Jeff Bezos from Amazon
- Joey Levin from IAC
‚Äã

Because I‚Äôm such an annual letter nerd, I was particularly excited to hit publish on Tiny‚Äôs second annual letter to shareholders last week.
‚Äã
‚ÄãIn it, Chris and I discuss:
‚Äã‚Äã
- Our first year as a public company
- 2023 performance across the group
- The six acquisitions we made this year
- An update on our $150M USD private fund
- Our thoughts on incentives and stock options
‚Äã
And much more. Hopefully we did the greats justice.
‚Äã
You can read the letter here.

‚Äç

Making things again feels unbelievably good. For the last three years, I‚Äôve been working on a book and it‚Äôs been one of the most satisfying projects I‚Äôve ever worked on. There‚Äôs something wonderful about crafting something physical‚Äîa creative act, a thing that will exist in the world for years, maybe decades, to come.
‚Äã
I used to create things every day. When I started my company almost twenty years ago, I was a designer. I‚Äôd put on headphones, drop into a flow state, and make something.
‚Äã
Mockups. Logos. Websites. Code. You name it.
‚Äã
But over the years, that happened less and less.
‚Äã
This is the paradox of scaling a business.
‚Äã
In the end, you no longer do the thing that started it all. The thing that brought you joy in the first place.
‚Äã
It feels a bit like going from a soccer player on the field, to the captain of the team, to the owner of the team up in the owner‚Äôs box.
‚Äã
You miss kicking the damn ball. The roaring crowd. Beers with the team afterwards.
‚Äã
Writing a book felt like kicking the ball again.
‚Äã
While I love business and all the problem solving that goes along with it, I‚Äôve come to realize that I need individual side projects that I can pour myself into.
‚Äã
That‚Äôs what this newsletter is: something creative and fun, where I don‚Äôt have to manage a team of people or field HR crises or negotiate raises.
‚Äã
I just turn on some good music and write for a couple hours.
‚Äã
And man does it ever feel good.
‚Äã
What‚Äôs yours?
‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã
‚ÄúThree thousand years ago a man thought that man might fly, and so he built himself wings, and Icarus‚Äîhis son‚Äîtrusting them and trying to fly, fell into the sea. Generations passed, and men said man would never fly, for it was not the will of God. And then man flew, and the agelong challenge of the bird was answered. Life is that which can hold a purpose for three thousand years and never yield. The individual fails, but life succeeds. The individual is foolish, but life holds in its blood and seed the wisdom of generations. The individual dies, but life, tireless and undiscourageable, goes on, wondering, longing, planning, trying, mounting, longing.‚Äù

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã
‚ÄúTry wearing it with a pair of brown desert boots, a stainless steel watch, and a pair of black sunglasses, with your hair a bit more casual.‚Äù
‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äã

‚Äç

My Vision Pro has been collecting dust on my kitchen island for the last few months. I‚Äôll admit, I wasn‚Äôt a fan when I first got one. To be honest, I‚Äôm still not. Until recently, the only use case I could see using it for was watching movies on planes
‚Äã
That is, until I tried Spacial FaceTime. Basically, you put on the Vision Pro, call a few friends on FaceTime, then tap a button to put it into ‚ÄúSpatial‚Äù mode.
‚Äã
I was sitting in my living room and suddenly, on the couch across from me, my friend Faisal‚Äôs ghostly head and ethereal body appeared. Sure, he looked like a very highly rendered video game character, but it was unmistakably him. Then Chris joined. Again, a perfect digital representation.
‚Äã
What astounded me the most was the eyes. It‚Äôs just insane. They move around perfectly, and you can make full natural eye contact with the people around you as you talk. It felt so much more personal than a Zoom call. We actually ended up hanging out and talking in my living room for almost an hour, and when I took off the headset I was shocked to realize that‚Ä¶I was sitting alone in my empty living room. My brain freaked out. It felt like they were there. Sure, they were weird ethereal ghosts, but it really felt like we were all sitting in the room together.
‚Äã
I can see so many amazing use cases for this. Hanging out with my kids or girlfriend virtually when I‚Äôm on the road. Playing board games with friends. Eventually, I‚Äôm sure there will be incredible ping-pong and tennis games. Imagine a virtual bar or social hangout where you can just walk up to people and start conversations. I think this could actually be a bit of a breakthrough‚Äîit just feels so much less lonely than texting and so much more intimate than a Zoom, where you‚Äôre just a box on a screen and you can‚Äôt make eye contact.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs not quite there yet. This future is coming in a few years, but it was a really amazing taste of what‚Äôs to come. I highly recommend you go check it out (you need the latest version 1.1 OS and to redo your Persona).
‚Äç

For years, I‚Äôve been searching for the perfect pair of mens jeans. I‚Äôm happy to announce I‚Äôve found my perfect match.
‚Äã
Most jeans are either groin-crushing, too stiff, too hot, or shrink unpredictably. After trying way, way too many (Japanese denim you have to hand wash, designer brands, Levis, custom made), I‚Äôve settled on Duer jeans. They look like normal jeans, but they feel like sweatpants. Insanely comfortable, stretchy, and cozy, without looking like a schmoe. Thanks to my friend Ben Moore for the recommendation.
‚Äç

If you‚Äôre an investor in a lot of businesses, funds, and asset classes, keeping track of it all is a pain in the butt. In the past, my team has used Excel and it‚Äôs been a nightmare. They‚Äôd spend hours each month manually updating values and it was a pain to keep up.
‚Äã
We recently switched to Kubera. Yes, there‚Äôs a bunch of ridiculously expensive solutions out there like Addepar. But want to know a secret? Addepar costs $100,000 a year. Kubera basically does the same thing and costs about $12/mo. It‚Äôs kind of insane.
‚Äã
TLDR: It‚Äôs a dashboard that shows you the current value (day-to-day) of all of your assets, presenting you with a breakdown of your current net worth, your investable cash position, and your assets broken down by class. It‚Äôs really great and has saved us a ton of time and money.
‚Äç

Ketosis is when your body switches from running on glucose (blood sugar) to ketones (fat). But to get there, takes days. You basically have to either starve yourself (prolonged fasting) or eat a ridiculously high fat (keto) diet, but people say it feels incredible. Many report feeling cognitively sharper and more focused, with lower anxiety and more consistent energy.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve tried Keto diets and fasting in the past, and I‚Äôve loved the feeling, but I find it difficult to do regularly. So, for the past few years I‚Äôve been experimenting with taking what are called exogenous ketones. Essentially, they are ‚Äúketone bodies‚Äù‚Äîthe things your body produces when it‚Äôs burning fat in ketosis‚Äîexcept you‚Äôre taking them orally. They immediately put you into a state of ketosis for 2-4 hours.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve been really enjoying Delta-G Tactical Ketones. I take them maybe once a week, but they are a great way to quickly get into the zone. (Note: they are 170 calories, so consume wisely and you want to avoid eating before/after taking them for a few hours.)
‚Äç

That‚Äôs all for this week‚Ä¶but one more thing. If you‚Äôre enjoying this, can you do me a favor and forward it to a friend? Thanks.

Now onto this week‚Äôs issue‚Ä¶

‚Äç

I hate getting long, rambling emails. In my opinion, most messages could be a few bullet points, but many people (especially those who have too much time on their hands and lawyers and other professionals) write endlessly detailed emails that take forever to parse.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve just solved this problem for myself, and it's insanely cool.
‚Äã
ChatGPT has a new feature called GPTs. Essentially, you can create custom ChatGPT instances that are pre-prompted to be particularly good at a specific skill, communicate in a certain way, or even trained on a body of material. For example, someone made a HubermanLab GPT that is trained on all of the podcast transcripts‚Äîsomething ChatGPT wouldn‚Äôt otherwise be able to answer accurately.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve played with this feature a little bit, but until recently hadn‚Äôt found it super useful‚Ä¶until I created my GPT Email Bot‚Ñ¢.
‚Äã
It‚Äôs super simple: I created a GPT that is trained on a bunch of sample emails that show my writing style. It‚Äôs instructed to:
‚Äã
1. Read the pasted email.
‚Äã
2. Ask me questions in a numbered list to address the key points the person is asking for feedback or answers on.
‚Äã
3. Use my answers to draft a response to the original pasted text as an email in my tone.
‚Äã
This thing is a miracle. I had a whole bunch of long, complicated emails from various lawyers and accountants sitting in my inbox that I‚Äôd been avoiding for weeks. Now I just paste them in and it asks me clear questions about what they need feedback/next steps on. I respond to the bullets (much faster than writing a thoughtful email) and it spits out a perfectly formatted, thoughtful email.
‚Äã
Before using this tool, I would:
‚Äã
1. Not respond for weeks, often slowing down critical projects.
‚Äã
2. When I did respond, I‚Äôd sound like a dick because I‚Äôd be super to the point. Now it looks like I‚Äôve written a beautiful, thoughtful, friendly email (but I haven‚Äôt).
‚Äã
Insanely useful.
‚Äã

The day you‚Äôve all been waiting for is finally here. We just launched colored AeroPress Clears and they are beautiful. To be honest, I wasn‚Äôt sure about this idea when I heard about it, but then I saw them. They turned out beautifully and add a nice dash of color to my kitchen. I particularly like the green. Check them out here.
‚Äã

I liked this quote from a recent interview with Mark Zuckerberg:
‚Äã
‚Äã‚ÄúMost of the time, when we make a decision that ends up seeming good, it‚Äôs because we messed something up before and we just didn't want to repeat the same mistake." ‚Äã
‚Äã
Even Zuck is just failing upwards ¬Ø\_(„ÉÑ)_/¬Ø
‚Äã

For some reason this popped into my head today:
‚Äã
‚ÄãOne of the worst kinds of businesses is one that sells something to a price sensitive consumer.
‚Äã
My nightmare business is a software company that creates software for school teachers to help them manage their class schedule.
‚Äã
Why? Because the ultimate customer is a teacher. Teachers don‚Äôt make much money, and are often price conscious. They also have to pay for it out of pocket.
‚Äã
A big ticket software purchase for a teacher might be $9.99 per month.
‚Äã
Software of the same complexity, in a different niche, for somebody who uses it to make a lot of money, could cost 100x the price. And usually they just throw it on a corporate credit card. They rarely pay for it themselves. Total price insensitivity.
‚Äã
Example:
‚Äã
Imagine if you instead made a simple software tool that helped hedge fund employees analyze a certain type of trade.
‚Äã
Or a niche tool that helped companies file some annoying government form that would deliver a $25,000 tax benefit.
‚Äã
‚ÄãNobody wakes up and thinks ‚ÄúI want to start that.‚Äù It‚Äôs freaking boring. And that‚Äôs why it‚Äôs a good business. You have a customer who will pay a large amount of money for you to solve their problem because it enables them to make far more, plus you have no competition.
‚Äã
Business on easy mode vs. hard mode.
‚Äã

Our old CFO, Ampere Chan, started a new advisory firm called Acorn. Ampere is insanely high integrity, a clear communicator, and a wonderful guy. If you need help sourcing, diligencing, or executing M&A deals, you should get in touch.

You are probably a jerk in some way.
April 12, 2024
Hey everyone,

If you're new here, welcome to Never Enough, where I brain dump interesting stuff that has been on my mind over the last week.

‚Äã

Today's edition is brought to you by InsideTracker*.

Ugh. Yes. I know. Another Tech Bro ‚Äúbiohacker‚Äù thing. But this one might have extended my life by a decade or more (because of it, I have reduced my risk of Alzheimer‚Äôs disease, heart attack, and stroke risk).

No, it doesn‚Äôt involve healing crystals or anti-aging supplements. This one is insanely simple:

Every 4-6 months for the last eight years, a phlebotomist comes to my house and draws some blood. It takes about 3 minutes.

I started using InsideTracker in 2016 and it sent me on a health journey that has resulted in me fixing a ton of underlying issues that I wasn‚Äôt even aware of.

For example because of my test results, I learned that:

I had APOe4, a genetic marker that significantly increases Alzheimer‚Äôs risk
I had insanely high cholesterol
My blood sugar was out of control
And much more. Look, you can go to your doctor and do these same tests. It will probably be cheaper. But you‚Äôll get a confusing printout of numbers that probably won‚Äôt make sense.

What I love about InsideTracker is that it makes blood test results insanely easy to understand and take action on. It provides specific recommendations based on what it sees, and can even incorporate data from your genetics, Apple Watch, and Oura Ring to custom tailor its recommendations. It also graphs out your results so you can track your progress over time.

Today, my blood looks markedly different from 2016 thanks to the insights and changes I made with InsideTracker. When a friend tells me they want to get their health in order, it‚Äôs the first place I send them.

Go check it out here. I‚Äôve done a special deal with them where you‚Äôll save 25% if you click this link.

‚Äã

Now onto this week‚Äôs issue‚Ä¶
‚Äã

I just launched my podcast. I‚Äôm fortunate to get to talk to interesting people for a living, and after 20 years of it I figured I should start recording some of these conversations. Go check out the first episode with my friend Sam Parr, host of My First Million, here:
‚Äã
‚ÄãSpotify / Apple Podcast / YouTube‚Äã
‚Äã
‚Äã
I treat myself like a drug addict. But not with drugs, with my phone.
‚Äã
I have no self-control. I‚Äôll often be enjoying a quiet moment reading a book or looking out at a view and suddenly, without even realizing it, I‚Äôm clawing for my phone in my pocket to check my Twitter feed.
‚Äã
After listening to this interview with Dr. Anna Lembke, I realized that you can become addicted‚Äîin the same sense you would to drugs or alcohol‚Äîto anything that stimulates dopamine.
‚Äã
This can be your phone. Chocolate cake. Fizzy water. You name it, if it stimulates dopamine, you‚Äôre hooped.
‚Äã
In the interview, she goes on to say that the effective treatment protocol for drugs and alcohol (6-8 weeks total abstinence) is the same way to treat things like phone or video game addiction. She talks about ‚Äúself-binding,‚Äù the forced removal of the stimuli from your environment.
‚Äã
Don‚Äôt want to eat chips? Don‚Äôt keep them in your house.
‚Äã
Well, the same applies to phones. What I ended up doing was going into my iPhone‚Äôs Screen Time settings, blocking all the distracting stuff that causes me stress or I scroll endlessly, then having my girlfriend set the passcode.
‚Äã
This means that, in order to use email, Twitter, check the news, Reddit, etc, I need to bug my girlfriend for the code.
‚Äã
As Dr. Lembke predicted, the first 6 weeks were tough, but now I don‚Äôt even miss them. And I prevent myself from going back down the rabbit hole by continuing to keep the aggressive Screen Time enabled.
‚Äã
I‚Äôve found that my life is much happier without scrolling feeds and current events. I‚Äôve managed to replace most of my scrolling time with reading books or long-form audio and I haven‚Äôt found that not having email, news, or social media at close hand has in any way negatively affected me.
‚Äã
You should try it.‚Äã
‚Äã
‚Äã
‚ÄúCivilization begins with order, grows with liberty, and dies with chaos.‚Äù‚Äã
‚Äã
That‚Äôs a line from one of the most profound books I‚Äôve ever read, The Lessons of History by Will and Ariel Durant. In just 128 pages, it summarizes the key lessons of human history in paired-down prose. Talk about cutting to the chase.
‚Äã
Here‚Äôs a few key ideas from the book:
History repeats, driven by unchanging human nature.
Geography molds civilizations' fates.
Class struggles fuel historical change.
Religion and morality shape societies.
Civilizations rise and fall in cycles.

My pal David Senra just did a great episode on this book on his (incredible) Founders podcast here. If you haven‚Äôt listened to Founders yet, you are missing out. Each week, he summarizes a great book and ties its lessons to other great books and leaders.
‚Äã
‚Äã
My friend Mark Nichols said something that struck me the other day:
‚Äã
‚Äã‚ÄúThere‚Äôs friends for reasons, for seasons, and for life.‚Äù‚Äã
‚Äã
Which are your friends?
‚Äã
‚Äã
Hiring a CEO is a weird skill. Not that many people have done it, and those who have have only done it once (most people either run their company for life or only pass the baton once and then retire). Chris and I have done it dozens of times, and my inbox is often packed with people asking me questions about how to transition their company to a CEO. I finally broke down and wrote a little PDF on it for my paid Twitter subscribers. If you want to check it out, go to my Twitter profile and click the purple ‚ÄúSubscribe‚Äù button, then check out this subscriber-only tweet.‚Äã
‚Äã
‚Äã
You are probably a jerk in some way. We all are.
‚Äã
But if you‚Äôre anything like me, you aren‚Äôt all that aware of it. You warp every situation to give yourself the most charitable interpretation. Our brains do our best to protect our egos. It's not fun to look at the darker sides of your personality.
‚Äã
This is a highly adaptive aspect of humanity. It enables us to get through life. But ignoring your flaws is no way to become the optimal version of yourself. At work, we all understand the value of 360 reviews. Deeply understanding how we are affecting those around us and how we can improve. Pain + reflection = progress.
‚Äã
A few years ago, my friend Mohnish Pabrai told me about a psychologist named Jack Skeen who had done a 360 review on his entire life. It sounded fascinating: he did a deep dive on every part of his day-to-day life. How he perceived himself. How his family and wife perceived him. His co-workers. His friends. As a result, he said that he changed his entire life.
‚Äã
It sounded kind of interesting, so I hired this guy, Dr. Jack Skeen.
‚Äã
He spoke with my closest friends, my wife, my co-workers, and we spent hours on the phone together.
‚Äã
He'd ask me weird uncomfortable questions like:
‚Äã
"Are you a good person?"
‚Äã
And
‚Äã
"What is your purpose?"
‚Äã
And
‚Äã
"Are you well liked?"
‚Äã
With long, awkward silences on the other end of the line to make me squirm, while he took notes.
‚Äã
I also did a series of multiple choice personality tests
‚Äã
All in all, it probably took 6-8 hours of my time, with another 10 hours of interviews across all my friends and colleagues.
‚Äã
What came out of it was brutal.
‚Äã
It was a complete picture of me...including all the horrible stuff. It was chock full of gut wrenching feedback and anonymized comments from the people who knew me best.
‚Äã
There were lots of positive things too, but it was a shit sandwich. Positives wrapped around the stuff nobody wants to read.
‚Äã
Here's a few:
‚Äã
"He is quite hard to work with. He is a terrible manager. He never gives positive feedback."
‚Äã
"He is oblivious of how he comes across to others."
‚Äã
"He needs to do the hard work of 'real' relationships. He can drop people when the work gets hard."
‚Äã
"Sometimes I feel like he's acting in social situations."
‚Äã
"I get the impression often that Andrew isn't happy."
‚Äã
As I read the report, I felt like I was going to hurl.
‚Äã
I immediately regretted it, and it put me into an existential tailspin. One of the key insights from the report was something I had felt but never quite put my finger on.
‚Äã
I scored high on the "histrionic" scale‚Ä¶
‚Äã
Something I had never heard of.
‚Äã
TLDR: Behaving in a histrionic manner = essentially being able to "perform" for others, craving attention, and wanting short term gratification.
‚Äã
Fortunately my score wasn't so high that it was a serious problem (for some people, it's a full on personality disorder and it ruins their lives), but it felt apt.
‚Äã
I'd noticed this in myself. That I could be dying inside, miserable, but when I needed to I could perform.
‚Äã
This was an incredibly valuable business skill. It helped me deeply connect with people and build positive relationships, but what I came to realize was that it was actually draining my life force. I'd arrive at a meeting I didn't want to go to and LIGHT UP, putting on my mask. Big smile. Firm handshake. Asking all the questions. Making the other person feel good. Tap dancing. Faking it.
‚Äã
But inside, I was secretly sad. I was being inauthentic. Something I had learned to do, for some reason, in my childhood.
‚Äã
I was an expert of wearing a mask, and it was making me profoundly sad.‚Äã‚Äã
There were a ton of other insights like this‚Äî some so personal I won't share to 20,000 people on the internet‚Äîbut it all culminated in me making huge changes to my life‚Ä¶‚Äã‚Äã
I got divorced.
I completely changed my day-to-day life.‚Äã
I started focusing on different things at work.
I changed the way I interacted with friends.
Stopped wearing the mask.
‚Äã
You name it, this psychological "Roadmap" I did with Jack Skeen rocked my world. It's been a few years now, but it's still one of the most impactful things I've ever done. It felt like 10 years of therapy in one month.
‚Äã
I know it sounds scary, but I highly recommend doing it yourself, if you can handle it: www.jackskeen.com‚Äã
‚Äã

‚Äã
